<div id="review_39202203" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39202203" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39202203?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394400266" href="/book/show/369050.A_Distant_Mirror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=medical">medical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2014-read">2014-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39202203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1865420745609983603">Tuchman's books are always interesting, but usually they have more than one can absorb. For this reason, reading them is always a bit of a struggle. OK, I am merely speaking for myself. <br/><br/>I am going to try to keep this review short, maybe a reaction to having just completed Tuchman's extensive opus. Not every detail need be explained. <i>A Distant Mirror</i> covers thoroughly every single aspect of medieval life. It covers in detail the battles of the Hundred Years' War. What is the Hundred Years' War?<br/><br/><i>Th</i></span>
  <span id="freeText1865420745609983603" style="display:none">Tuchman's books are always interesting, but usually they have more than one can absorb. For this reason, reading them is always a bit of a struggle. OK, I am merely speaking for myself. <br /><br />I am going to try to keep this review short, maybe a reaction to having just completed Tuchman's extensive opus. Not every detail need be explained. <i>A Distant Mirror</i> covers thoroughly every single aspect of medieval life. It covers in detail the battles of the Hundred Years' War. What is the Hundred Years' War?<br /><br /><i>The Hundred Years' War was a series of conflicts waged from 1337 to 1453 pitting the House of Plantagenet, rulers of the Kingdom of England, against the House of Valois for control of the Kingdom of France. Each side drew many allies into the war.....<br /><br />Historians commonly divide the war into three phases separated by truces: 1) the Edwardian Era War (1337–1360); 2) the Caroline War (1369–1389); and 3) the Lancastrian War (1415–1453).<br /><br />Contemporary conflicts in neighbouring areas, which were directly related to this conflict, included the War of the Breton Succession (1341–1364), the Castilian Civil War (1366–1369), the War of the Two Peters (1356–1375) in Aragon, and the 1383–85 Crisis in Portugal. <u>Later historians invented the term "Hundred Years' War" as a periodization to encompass all of these events</u>, thus constructing the longest military conflict in history.</i><br /><br />That is taken directly from Wiki. Pay attention to the sentence I have underlined. This is a book where the majority of pages are concerned with war and battles. Tuchman has chosen to follow one man of nobility through his lifetime, Enguerrand de Coucy VII(1340-1397). He is from Picardy, France, and is married to the daughter of the Kind of England. He is a perfect character to follow since he is thus connected to both the French and English nobility, the two warring nations. He took part in many of the decisive battles. The book follows what he DOES. Little attempt is made to understand the psychology of the man. That is not the point of the book. You observe his actions. Who does he marry? What battles did he fight in? Where did he live? How did he die? Through him we study medieval life and the Hundred Years’ War. After his death Tuchman quickly summarizes the end of the Hundred Years' War. So while the Edwardian Era War and the Caroline War are depicted in complete detail as well as related battles with the Bretons, battles in Italy, in Spain, in Belgium and finally in Bulgaria (contemporary country names used), only a quick summary of the Lancastrian War is given. Enguerrand dies in 1397 at the Battle of Nicopolis in Bulgaria. This explains why the Lancastrian War is summarized, in the epilog.<br /><br /><br />Approximately the first fourth of the book establishes the setting. Enguerrand was born in 1340. The world he was born into, that is the earlier years of the 14th Century prior to his birth are studied. They provide a general overview of how people behaved and thought during the medieval era, while the remainder of the book covers more closely the battles. I preferred the first part of the book. The Black Death, the Schism of the Church, clothing, foods, mysticism, chivalry and how motherhood was perceived – it’s all here and it is all interesting, but there is too much to grasp given the abundance of details. <br /><br />I listened to the audiobook narrated by Nadia May. This was excellent, but I do NOT recommend the audiobook. There are so many names of places and people! it is hard to keep everything straight. You need maps and genealogical charts which a paper book can easily provide. <br /><br />I learned a lot. It is an excellent book, but in terms of my personal enjoyment I can only say I liked it. I don’t love books describing battles.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1865420745609983603" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_548339563" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548339563" />
    <a title="Matt Brady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7881793-matt-brady"><img alt="Matt Brady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404212164p2/7881793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548339563?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt Brady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt Brady" href="/user/show/7881793-matt-brady">Matt Brady</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7881793-matt-brady?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7881793-matt-brady?shelf=medieval-history">medieval-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7881793-matt-brady?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7881793-matt-brady?shelf=hundred-years-war">hundred-years-war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7881793-matt-brady?shelf=female-author">female-author</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer548339563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10030205403470401022">The Hundred Years War, the Papal Schism, the Black Death, peasant uprisings, the death of chivalry, crusades, assassinations, tournaments, all these things and more Tuchman explores through an examination of the life of one man, Enguerrand de Coucy. Scion of perhaps the most powerful and wealthiest baronial family in France, Coucy lead a fairly amazing life. He fought wars in his homeland of France, Italy, North Africa, Switzerland and Bulgaria, lead important diplomatic missions, twice turned d</span>
  <span id="freeText10030205403470401022" style="display:none">The Hundred Years War, the Papal Schism, the Black Death, peasant uprisings, the death of chivalry, crusades, assassinations, tournaments, all these things and more Tuchman explores through an examination of the life of one man, Enguerrand de Coucy. Scion of perhaps the most powerful and wealthiest baronial family in France, Coucy lead a fairly amazing life. He fought wars in his homeland of France, Italy, North Africa, Switzerland and Bulgaria, lead important diplomatic missions, twice turned down the title of Constable of France and, for over a decade, was married to the favourite daughter of the King of England, who also happened to be his captor at the time, and died a captive of the Ottoman Sultan after the disastrous Battle of Nicopolis.<br /><br />This isn't a strict biography though, and Tuchman wanders wherever her interests take her. There's a hell of a lot of ground to cover, so this isn't as tight as, say, <i>Guns of August</i>, and Tuchman will occasionally get hung up on something, but these are minor faults. Speaking of getting hung up, this book was cited by George R R Martin and the inspiration he drew from it is very apparent. Tuchman really relishes describing feasts, fetes and tournaments in incredible detail, and portrays the major and minor figures of the era with a blend of real ambiguity, grittiness and the occasional larger-than-life anecdote that any reader of A Song of Ice and Fire will find familiar. This book is littered with Barristan Selmys and Gregor Cleganes.<br /><br />I particularly enjoyed Tuchman's sneering portrayal of knighthood and the ruling class. No flower of chivalry here, Tuchman portrays people like the famous Black Prince as the brutal, rapacious, violent thugs they really were, and even her main character, who she is obviously fond of, is not spared, being described as "the least compromised of his class and kind by brutality, venality and reckless indulgence." Not exactly a glowing reccomendation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10030205403470401022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_131386174" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131386174" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652278-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224862599p2/1652278.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131386174?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/1652278-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131386174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18211181328173004890">I read a little more than half of this a couple of years ago and stopped. This time I read it all, for the discussion of my local book group. I really liked it--I've never NOT liked a Tuchman book. I admire the way she's able to follow one historical figure and still manage to tell the story of a whole age, especially one person, in this case Enguerrand de Coucy about whom so little is known other than what he did. There exist references to him in contemporary works but never more than a figure </span>
  <span id="freeText18211181328173004890" style="display:none">I read a little more than half of this a couple of years ago and stopped. This time I read it all, for the discussion of my local book group. I really liked it--I've never NOT liked a Tuchman book. I admire the way she's able to follow one historical figure and still manage to tell the story of a whole age, especially one person, in this case Enguerrand de Coucy about whom so little is known other than what he did. There exist references to him in contemporary works but never more than a figure who steps out of the background now and then to be seen from a distance. He nevertheless comes alive, particularly toward the end when he seems to be a thoughtful and sensible man in a era which encouraged the opposite. I also liked the book because I know so little about France and am trying to rectify that lack. And before this "the hundred years war" seemed to me an historical stalwart with no detail behind it--except perhaps the picture of Mother Courage pulling her cart.... I know much more English history so it was instructive to see the Black Prince and John of Gaunt and the Peasant Rebellion from the other side and in the context of what was going on on the continent.<br /><br />The focus on Coucy was not like Tuchman's focus on Stillwell in her book on World War II in China. That really was a biography of Stillwell as well as an attempt to understand the US in China in WWII and after. Coucy is, in many ways, a rack--a hat rack not a torture device-- to hang Tuchman's understanding of the 14th century on. Her title in interesting: "distant" is self explanatory--600 years and more ago, but why "mirror"? That I think is because Tuchman found the calamitous 14th century (her term) analogous in many ways to her own century. Understanding that pivotal century might be "consoling in a period of similar disarray" characterized by war, plague, religious schism, irrationality and progress.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18211181328173004890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2903530855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2903530855" />
    <a title="Janet Roger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99920177-janet-roger"><img alt="Janet Roger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584405041p2/99920177.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2903530855?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janet Roger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janet Roger" href="/user/show/99920177-janet-roger">Janet Roger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/99920177-janet-roger?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2903530855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4717488736763673832">One way to get a grip on some history is to take on the personalities of the day. Read around Richard III and - love him or hate him - along the way you’re not only going to get into the foreground detail of the Wars of the Roses, you’ll begin making some sense of the backstory of the Plantaget dynasty. It’s the same with Barbara Tuchman’s terrific idea of approaching the war and politics of 14th century Europe, through just one man - Enguerrand de Coucy - whose lifetime fits so perfectly with t</span>
  <span id="freeText4717488736763673832" style="display:none">One way to get a grip on some history is to take on the personalities of the day. Read around Richard III and - love him or hate him - along the way you’re not only going to get into the foreground detail of the Wars of the Roses, you’ll begin making some sense of the backstory of the Plantaget dynasty. It’s the same with Barbara Tuchman’s terrific idea of approaching the war and politics of 14th century Europe, through just one man - Enguerrand de Coucy - whose lifetime fits so perfectly with the story she wants to relate. It’s a stroke of near-genius, and one that brings an intricate account to sparkling life. There is, after all, so much to tell. The towering, imperious Enguerrand is her path to a sweeping review of international power struggle, intrigue and bloody conflict; the persecutions and plagues, dynastic marriages, petty bickering and shattering squabbles of a bare-knuckle century. In this landscape, no-one escapes the fallout when great names act. The history grips like a vice. The writing of it in A Distant Mirror is magnificent. Read anything Barbara Tuchman puts her pen to. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4717488736763673832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67691403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1490061783112430162">I have been a Tuchman fan for years but put off reading this book because it concerned a period of history of which I was not particularly interested. Wrong!!! Chock full of details, it fills in all the details of a bloody, unenlightened time in history where war for no justifiable reason was the norm, crusades against distant lands were the epitome of a knight's duty, and the Black Death was decimating half the world's population.<br/>As usual, the author has done extensive research and although it </span>
  <span id="freeText1490061783112430162" style="display:none">I have been a Tuchman fan for years but put off reading this book because it concerned a period of history of which I was not particularly interested. Wrong!!! Chock full of details, it fills in all the details of a bloody, unenlightened time in history where war for no justifiable reason was the norm, crusades against distant lands were the epitome of a knight's duty, and the Black Death was decimating half the world's population.<br />As usual, the author has done extensive research and although it is often difficult to keep all the players straight, it offers a fascinating panoply of a time when "knighthood was in flower". I'm sorry I waited so long to read it.</span>

<div id="review_1891983840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Chris Gager" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager"><img alt="Chris Gager" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307204529p2/5295008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1891983840?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gager" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gager" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager">Chris Gager</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394400266" href="/book/show/369050.A_Distant_Mirror">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1891983840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11937661845092405579">Just started. Looks interesting ...<br/><br/>Well, it IS interesting, but kind of dry, as at least one other reviewer has noted. It's going to take a while!<br/><br/>Continuing to enjoy this book as the author is doing a fine job of combining the informative with reading pleasure. So far it's a bit like reading a sci-fi account of a far-away alien planet and it's weird but familiar culture! Jack Vance-like indeed ...<br/><br/>The Black Plague - why can't we have one of those, only more virulent and untreatable? Steve King ha</span>
  <span id="freeText11937661845092405579" style="display:none">Just started. Looks interesting ...<br /><br />Well, it IS interesting, but kind of dry, as at least one other reviewer has noted. It's going to take a while!<br /><br />Continuing to enjoy this book as the author is doing a fine job of combining the informative with reading pleasure. So far it's a bit like reading a sci-fi account of a far-away alien planet and it's weird but familiar culture! Jack Vance-like indeed ...<br /><br />The Black Plague - why can't we have one of those, only more virulent and untreatable? Steve King had the right idea in "The Stand." The cure for the many man-made ills the rest of the planet is suffering from could be a plague with a 99.6% non-survival rate.<br /><br />Moving along now into post-Poitiers(a disaster for the French) brigandage. Sounds like the 14th c. was a miserable time to be living(and dying). Sometimes you're just trapped. Not a great time to be a poor "villein"(peasant) either - of course!<br /><br />I was at the local transfer station, where I voluntarily straighten out the book shelves(and get some good ones for myself), and I found a nice hardbound edition of this book. It'll be easier to read than the paperback, so that one will go into the out pile. However, the paperback is better in that the cover image(of an impressive Medieval painting) is in color. My newly acquired hardbound is blank of course. The page numbers correspond exactly ...<br /><br />Part One ends with the papal schism thing. Part Two begins tonight.<br /><br />Lately reading of peasant uprisings in England and on the Continent. Sheesh! Has it not ever been thus? Are we not still fighting the same political/economic battles in this country? The haves versus the have-nots. Republicans versus Democrats. My reading of various history books(this one, "Citizens" by Simon Schama(about the Fr. Rev.), and "The Glory and the Dream"(about the middle of the American 20th century) all feature the same thing. The rich and powerful(and now the corporate) keep wanting more and everyone else getting the shaft. Those f---ers NEVER GIVE UP! The rest of us mean NOTHING to them. When oh when will the rest of the people in this country get that?????<br /><br />Getting towards the end as we reach the 1380's of this action-packed and seemingly chaotic century. SO MUCH detail to attend to, but it's still fascinating. The violence(warfare) is endless and usually no clear outcomes ensue. Just more warfare and intrigue.<br /><br />Finished up last night with this excellent and engrossing read. Yes, there IS an abundance of detail, but then, there was an abundance of dramatic activity during this dysfunctional epoch. As Ms. Tuchman points out, things DID begin to turn around for Europe in the latter half of the 15 c. Whew!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11937661845092405579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5030911199217029675">This introduction to the 14th century uses the biographical framework of the life of the seigneur of Coucy to take in aspects of the hundred years war, the crusade of Nicopolis and late medieval life generally.<br/><br/>That's what the book is and that is the problem I suspect, in that the Tuchman style much loved in great fat book about the First World War and the period preceding it does not work in a period without the same wealth of information, you can not recapture the sense of immediacy and driving</span>
  <span id="freeText5030911199217029675" style="display:none">This introduction to the 14th century uses the biographical framework of the life of the seigneur of Coucy to take in aspects of the hundred years war, the crusade of Nicopolis and late medieval life generally.<br /><br />That's what the book is and that is the problem I suspect, in that the Tuchman style much loved in great fat book about the First World War and the period preceding it does not work in a period without the same wealth of information, you can not recapture the sense of immediacy and driving rapid narrative without the mass of detail to illustrate an intense story taking place over a short length of time. The era of the Black Death, has its drama but it is rather spread out, and while the seigneur of Coucy was a real person, we can't feel that we know him or have been with him on life's journey for all Tuchman's literary skills. </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer528534439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9610529166126642284">Beutifully written and very detailed book. Recommended to all people genuinely interested in the history of the period. It is not about knights in gleaming armour rescuing and seducing defenseless ladies, but about a potent and credible mixture of well researched historical truth and good story-telling. A classic. If you want to get a good understanding of 14 Century Europe this is a book for you (it gets a little long and dense at times, but overall it is a rewarding read). </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16638183316036836081">Just got a nice hardbound copy of this for Christmas, so I'm set to read it again ...<br/><br/>My dad is a Barbara Tuchman fan, so I grew up around this book. As a small child, I used to ponder with interest the scary cover art, which shows the arrival of the Forth Horseman of the Apocalypse ("and his name was death" for you Johnny Cash fans). I finally read the book when I was in high school, and I have reread it several times since. It is a perfect example of good history writing - absolutely engaging a</span>
  <span id="freeText16638183316036836081" style="display:none">Just got a nice hardbound copy of this for Christmas, so I'm set to read it again ...<br /><br />My dad is a Barbara Tuchman fan, so I grew up around this book. As a small child, I used to ponder with interest the scary cover art, which shows the arrival of the Forth Horseman of the Apocalypse ("and his name was death" for you Johnny Cash fans). I finally read the book when I was in high school, and I have reread it several times since. It is a perfect example of good history writing - absolutely engaging and human. <br /><br />Among other things, this book, along with "Rats, Lice &amp; History," crystalized my interest in the history of disease and epidemiology (you are right to ask - is there anything I'm not interested in?). If you're ever stuck home with a bad flu, I recommend reading the chapter on the Black Death - it really puts things in perspective.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15817489256720981068">While I mostly enjoyed reading Tuchman's <b>comprehensive</b> book on the 14th Century, it was TMI. I'm not writing a thesis nor a college paper. <br/><br/>I read this for the enjoyment of knowing more about the century when the Black Plague decimated the world. Well, I got that and much much more.   <span id="freeText15817489256720981068" style="display:none">While I mostly enjoyed reading Tuchman's <b>comprehensive</b> book on the 14th Century, it was TMI. I'm not writing a thesis nor a college paper. <br /><br />I read this for the enjoyment of knowing more about the century when the Black Plague decimated the world. Well, I got that and much much more. I read this for fun, I thought, however it was about twice as long and twice the information as I wanted and/or needed. <br /><br />Unfortunately for me it got tiresome and although I did learn a lot such as the size of a royal baby blanket, 15 feet X 30 feet, I think much of the information was simply useless for my purpose. <br /><br />Now I'm wondering whatever will I do with that royal baby blanket information? Oh, I know, forget it---unless someone asks. <br /> </span>

<div id="review_1446974529" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1446974529" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1446974529?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307291608" href="/book/show/7016164-a-distant-mirror">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to B. P. by: </span>
      Ta-Nehisi Coates
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=history-stuff">history-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=non-fiction-stuff">non-fiction-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=watching-history-pale">watching-history-pale</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1446974529" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11457612155626453038">"<i>If the [century] seemed full of brilliance and adventure to a few at the top, to most they were a succession of wayward dangers; of the three galloping evils: pillage, plague, and taxes; of fierce and tragic conflicts, bizarre fates, capricious money, sorcery, betrayals, insurrections, murder, madness, and the downfall of princes; of dwindling labor for the fields, of cleared land reverting to waste; and always the recurring black shadow of pestilence carrying its message of guilt and sin and t</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11457612155626453038" style="display:none">"<i>If the [century] seemed full of brilliance and adventure to a few at the top, to most they were a succession of wayward dangers; of the three galloping evils: pillage, plague, and taxes; of fierce and tragic conflicts, bizarre fates, capricious money, sorcery, betrayals, insurrections, murder, madness, and the downfall of princes; of dwindling labor for the fields, of cleared land reverting to waste; and always the recurring black shadow of pestilence carrying its message of guilt and sin and the hostility of God.<br />Mankind was not improved by the message. Consciousness of wickedness made behavior worse. Violence threw off restraints. It was a time of default. Rules crumbled, institutions failed in their functions. Knighthood did not protect; the Church, more worldly than spiritual, did not guide the way to God; towns, once the agents of progress and the commonweal, were absorbed in mutual hostilities and divided by class war; the population, depleted by the Black Death, did not recover. The War of England and France and the brigandage it spawned revealed the emptiness of chivalry's military pretensions and the falsity of its moral ones. The [Papal] schism shook the foundations of the central institution, spreading a deep and pervasive uneasiness. People felt subject to events beyond their control, swept, like flotsam at sea, hither and yon in a universe without reason or purpose. They lived through a period which suffered and struggled without visible advance. They longed for a remedy, for a revival of faith, for stability and order that never came.</i>" <br /><br /><br />Don't think I can do better to summarize the topic of the book than the above quote, so that frees me to give general impressions and a sort-of explanation of why I have read it and will be turning my attention to more books on European history for the foreseeable future. <br /><br />This book (and historian) was put to my attention by one <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1214964.Ta_Nehisi_Coates" title="Ta-Nehisi Coates" rel="nofollow noopener">Ta-Nehisi Coates</a>, who read it for similar reasons I have read it. It's charting of the decline of the medieval era is breath-taking. Tuchman, who is known more for her books on 20th century warfare, did an amazing job here. She has written an engaging, down-to-earth history, of the 14th century. Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8439908.Edward_E__Baptist" title="Edward E. Baptist" rel="nofollow noopener">Edward E. Baptist</a>, she recounts the history but is not afraid to commentate on the folly of it. She uses the life of a French nobleman--Enguerrand VII, Sire (Lord) of Coucy &amp; Count of Soissons--to tell the greater story of this time of woe. Rarely do historical eras transition peacefully and the end of the Middle Ages was a very violent 150 years. Though it did have some interesting writers like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/72460.Francesco_Petrarca" title="Francesco Petrarca" rel="nofollow noopener">Petrarch</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1838.Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer" rel="nofollow noopener">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/29227.Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio" rel="nofollow noopener">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/242147.William_Langland" title="William Langland" rel="nofollow noopener">William Langland</a>, it was a time of plague and nearly every person with authority being enslaved to the worst myths of chivalry and their own egos. The three most capable heads of state during the century were King Edward III of England, King Charles V of France, and Duke Gian Galeazzo Visconti of Milan. They weren't "good guys," because such does not exist in the politics of any age, but they were by far the most competent in their prime. The one factor in their favor was that they either subverted or (in the case of Charles V) did not subscribe to the chivalric code--the dominant and destructive ideology of the era. The Sire de Coucy's success during the century was in-part because he only used chivalry as a last resort in all matters. His reliance on practical wisdom and personal charisma saved him from the brutal fate of many of his contemporaries, but his obedience to the code at the end of the century during the Nicopolis Crusade cost him his life. <br /><br />Here is a clip of Tuchman being interviewed on the book and some of her other works: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://archive.org/details/openmind_ep1405">https://archive.org/details/openmind_...</a><br /><br />My reason for reading this (and my plan for reading more European history) is a rather simple one. In becoming ever more versed in history and growing up being educated in African-American history and <s>white-American</s> mainstream/general-American history, I felt like I was seeing a picture, but not fully. I've spent almost thirty years of my life seeing the "effect," now I want to know the "cause." Sun Tzu says " know your enemy, know yourself." <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8113026.Conversations_With_Myself" title="Conversations With Myself by Nelson Mandela" rel="nofollow noopener">I read Nelson Mandela's story</a> of studying the works and language of the Afrikaners in jail to better understand the people who he was dealing with. So I have taken-up this challenge. When one reads the news and sees what's happening now, it becomes apparent that the mirror is much closer than it was when Tuchman wrote this book. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11486.The_Color_Purple" title="The Color Purple by Alice Walker" rel="nofollow noopener">The Color Purple</a>, the protagonist tells the antagonist that everything he's done to her, he's already done to himself. My educated-guess is that that's true here too, given the treatment of villens and Jews described in this book.<br /><br />I had thought to put more and make this a thorough review, but I think this sums-up my thoughts directly, anymore is just wasting all our time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11457612155626453038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_9665006" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9665006" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/545336-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329191662p2/545336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9665006?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/545336-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394400266" href="/book/show/369050.A_Distant_Mirror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=mid-east">mid-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=military">military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9665006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15699423639268446246">Look around you, your friends, family, neighborhood, school, town, state, country. Now imagine 40-50% of them are gone forever. Empty chairs, rooms, houses, fields, roads, etc. What you see is what happened in the 14th Century as the Black Death, war, brigandage, famine, poverty ravaged the population of Europe. Tuchman brings this world alive with incredible detail, following the story of one French nobleman. Not always riveting reading but well worth the effort—it took me over a month to final</span>
  <span id="freeText15699423639268446246" style="display:none">Look around you, your friends, family, neighborhood, school, town, state, country. Now imagine 40-50% of them are gone forever. Empty chairs, rooms, houses, fields, roads, etc. What you see is what happened in the 14th Century as the Black Death, war, brigandage, famine, poverty ravaged the population of Europe. Tuchman brings this world alive with incredible detail, following the story of one French nobleman. Not always riveting reading but well worth the effort—it took me over a month to finally slog through. The final battle against the Turks was emblematic of the entire period, hubris and the vanity of the so-called nobility is shattered on the battlefield. So much death and destruction. I won’t attempt a detailed review of the book—there are so many good ones already. Just a few excerpts that I found especially interesting. Overall <b> 5 Stars</b> but at times seems like homework rather than fun.<br /><br />Tuchman’s law is now multiplied by a factor of 24/7 X 365 with the advent of talking heads on cable: <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i> Disaster is rarely as pervasive as it seems from recorded accounts. The fact of being on the record makes it appear continuous and ubiquitous whereas it is more likely to have been sporadic both in time and place. Besides, persistence of the normal is usually greater than the effect of disturbance, as we know from our own times. After absorbing the news of today, one expects to face a world consisting entirely of strikes, crimes, power failures, broken water mains, stalled trains, school shutdowns, muggers, drug addicts, neo-Nazis, and rapists. The fact is that one can come home in the evening-on a lucky day-without having encountered more than one or two of these phenomena. This has led me to formulate Tuchman’s Law, as follows: <b>“The fact of being reported multiplies the apparent extent of any deplorable development by five to tenfold” (or any figure the reader would care to use)”</b></i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Illuminating explanation of the eventual corruption of the Catholic Church in the Middle Ages:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i> By Constantine’s gift, Christianity was both officially established and fatally compromised. As William Langland wrote, <br /><br /><b>When the kindness of Constantine gave Holy Church endowments <br />In lands and leases, lordships and servants, <br />The Romans heard an angel cry on high above them, <br />“This day dos ecclesiae has drunk venom <br />And all who have Peter’s power are poisoned forever.” </b><br /><br />That conflict between the reach for the divine and the lure of earthly things was to be the central problem of the Middle Ages. The claim of the Church to spiritual leadership could never be made wholly credible to all its communicants when it was founded in material wealth. </i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The idea of chivalry is much exaggerated. This Century was filled with bloodthirsty men with only one skill:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i> …knight-chevalier in French-meant the man on horseback. “A brave man mounted on a good horse,” it was acknowledged, “can do more in an hour of fighting than ten or maybe one hundred could do afoot.” The destrier or war-horse was bred to be “strong, fiery, swift, and faithful” and ridden only in combat. Enroute the knight rode his palfrey, high-bred but of quieter disposition, while his squire led the destrier at his right hand-hence its name, from dexter. In fulfilling military service, horse and knight were considered inseparable; without a mount the knight was a mere man. <br /><br />Battle was his exaltation. “If I had one foot already in Paradise,” exclaimed Garin li Loherains, the hero of a chanson de geste, “I would withdraw it to go and fight!” The troubadour Bertrand de Born, himself a noble, was more explicit. <br /><br /><b>My heart is filled with gladness when I see <br />Strong castles besieged, stockades broken and overwhelmed, many vassals struck down, <br />Horses of the dead and wounded roving at random. <br />And when battle is joined, let all men of good lineage <br />Think of naught but the breaking of heads and arms, <br />For it is better to die than be vanquished and live. <br />I tell you I have no such Joy as when I hear the shout <br />“On! On!” from both sides and the neighing of riderless steeds, And groans of ‘‘Help me! Help me!” <br />And when I see both great and small <br />Fall in the ditches and on the grass <br />And see the dead transfixed by spear shafts! <br />Lords, mortgage your domains, castles, cities, <br />But never give up war! </b><br /><br />Dante pictured Bertrand in Hell, carrying his severed head before him as a lantern. </i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />These medieval people had a way with words and were not afraid of a little sexual innuendo:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i> Isabella could well have listened to the tales of Jean de Condé, poet in her lifetime at her mother’s native court in Hainault. His style is illustrated by a story about a game of truth-telling played at court before a tournament. A knight, asked by the Queen if he has fathered any children, is forced to admit he has not, and indeed he <b>“did not have the look of a man who could please his mistress when he held her naked in his arms. For his beard was…little more than the kind of fuzz that ladies have in certain places.”</b> The Queen tells him she does not doubt his word, <b>“for it is easy to judge from the state of the hay whether the pitchfork is any good.”</b> In his turn, the knight asks, <b>“Lady, answer me without deceit. Is there hair between your legs?”</b> When she replies, <b>“None at all,”</b> he comments, <b>“Indeed I do believe you, for grass does not grow on a well-beaten path.”</b> </i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The third wave of the Plague resulted in extreme measures in some places:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i> With the third advent of the plague, contagion was more strictly controlled if no better understood. While it raged in Milan, Bernabo ordered every victim to be taken out of the city and left to die or recover in the fields. Any person who nursed a plague patient was to be strictly quarantined for ten days; priests were to examine their parishioners for symptoms and report to a special commission under pain of death for failure; anyone who brought the disease into the city was subject to the death penalty and confiscation of property. Venice denied entry to all ships suspected of carrying infection, but with the flea and rat not yet implicated, the precautions, though groping in the right direction, failed to stop the carrier. At Piacenza, where Coucy’s war effort ended, half the population died, and at Pisa, where the plague lasted two years, it was said to have wiped out four fifths of the children. </i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I thought Tuchman was very effective in explaining the Hundred Years’ War and all the conflicts around the continent. With all the battles and wars, you would think the knights and leaders would gain some expertise in going to war, but they don’t seem to: <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i> Although Anjou had proclaimed his intention to <b>“promote the fate of the Church by the force of chivalry”</b>-that is, by force of arms-he failed to exert that force against Urban. Leaving the coast at Ancona to cross the Apennines early in September, he bypassed the road to Rome, although a bold effort might have taken the city at this time. Agents had brought word that Hawkwood’s White Company, promised for Urban’s defense, had been held back by Florence for her own protection. Instead, against the advice of Amadeo of Savoy, Anjou took the lower road for Naples, and as the army passed through defiles and across gorges, between peaks “that touched the sky,” calamity overtook them. Highland brigands, pulled by strings from Naples, attacked the baggage train and the rear guard escorting the treasure, with the result that Anjou arrived at Caserta, within a day’s march of Naples, very much poorer than when he set out. Reconnoitering terrain in advance was not part of medieval warfare because it was not part of tournaments. The clash was everything. </i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The Germans in WWI destroyed a great castle that helped focus the story in Tuchman’s book:<br /><i>In 1917 Picardy, invaded once more, had been occupied for three years by the German army. Prince Rupprecht of Bavaria, commander of the Sixth Army, urged General Ludendorff, Chief of General Staff, to ensure that the castle of Coucy be spared as a unique architectural treasure of no current military value. Neither side, he pointed out, had attempted to use it for military purposes, and its destruction “would only mean a blow to our own prestige quite uselessly.” Ludendorff did not like appeals to culture. Coucy having been unwisely called to his attention, he decided to make it an example of superior values. Rammed with 28 tons of explosives at his orders, the colossus raised by Enguerrand III in the age of the greatest builders since Greece and Rome was dynamited to the ground. </i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15699423639268446246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review9665006">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer589248453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7436596952549292165">I have to take a bit of a different tact on this book than many other reviewers. Let me start off by saying that I liked the book. I'm not going to say I really liked it because I found it a bit dry. And when I say dry, I mean I was losing my place, or forgetting what I'd just read. <br/><br/>One of the reasons for this is that there are so many people, so many place names, and so many goings-on. It's hard to keep track of all of that! Even looking at the 'Look Inside' on Amazon right now, I can see tons </span>
  <span id="freeText7436596952549292165" style="display:none">I have to take a bit of a different tact on this book than many other reviewers. Let me start off by saying that I liked the book. I'm not going to say I really liked it because I found it a bit dry. And when I say dry, I mean I was losing my place, or forgetting what I'd just read. <br /><br />One of the reasons for this is that there are so many people, so many place names, and so many goings-on. It's hard to keep track of all of that! Even looking at the 'Look Inside' on Amazon right now, I can see tons of names in the first chapter. <br /><br />Nonetheless, you get a good picture of this time from a good historian. I should read it again some day, and probably will.</span>

<div id="review_381640609" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/381640609" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/381640609?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=cultural-studies-and-other">cultural-studies-and-other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=14th-centurylit">14th-centurylit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer381640609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5681363302383346082"><a href='http://www.cartoonstock.com/directory/t/triggered.asp' rel='nofollow noopener'>This</a> is what I thought the Hundred Years' War was all about. Apparently that's wrong. (Or maybe <i>Tuchman</i> is wrong, hmmm?)<br/><br/>We pretty much all know what the Middle Ages was all about, we all have at least heard tell of the Hundred Years' War or the Black Death or the Papal Schism. Those terms are all familiar. What Tuchman did here was bring all of those familiar terms to life. She filled in the gaps that public education doesn't (whether due to funding or time or the Board of Education doesn't thin</span>
  <span id="freeText5681363302383346082" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.cartoonstock.com/directory/t/triggered.asp" rel="nofollow noopener">This</a> is what I thought the Hundred Years' War was all about. Apparently that's wrong. (Or maybe <i>Tuchman</i> is wrong, hmmm?)<br /><br />We pretty much all know what the Middle Ages was all about, we all have at least heard tell of the Hundred Years' War or the Black Death or the Papal Schism. Those terms are all familiar. What Tuchman did here was bring all of those familiar terms to life. She filled in the gaps that public education doesn't (whether due to funding or time or the Board of Education doesn't think it's as important, whatever), so for myself there were a few "ah-<i>ha</i>!" kinds of moments. (Like "Oh, so the War <i>didn't</i> start because of a wedgie!")<br /><br />The additional bonus of reading Tuchman is that she wasn't a dry writer. She did what seems like a thousand years' worth of research and managed to make just about every word of it absolutely fascinating. Yes, my eyes glazed over a time or two; I was in this read primarily to know more about the society, culture, and day-to-day living of folks back in the Late Middle Ages. The battles were of less interest to me, though kudos to Tuchman for the amount of detail she provided. She had that research down to bitch-slaps and other offenses. There are 18 pages of Bibliography and something like 36 pages of Notes. She didn't just make this stuff up here. She did a lot of research, and I appreciate that.<br /><br />So the parts that I signed up to read were especially fascinating to me - how women were treated in society, how so-and-so died (like in a fire after being wrapped in booze-soaked wraps to help his fever - whoops!), the plague and sickness - y'know, totally gruesome stuff like that. It's not a quick read, though it's not a difficult read, if that makes any sense. Most readers will get at least something out of this, no matter what they're going in expecting or wanting. I wanted one thing but came out with so much more. It's action-packed, written in a narrative that doesn't exclude or alienate the reader, and (again!) so freaking well-researched it's insane.<br /><br />This was my first Tuchman, but I have more in my stacks. I'm looking forward to reading those others even more now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5681363302383346082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1347840969" />
    <a title="Jeanette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette"><img alt="Jeanette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372095557p2/11869072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1347840969?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette">Jeanette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1347840969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4779167864699282362">This is just a reaction for the 4 or 5 chapter revisit I made back to this excellent history. Having read the entire decades ago, and also having used it for some reference referrals for others at different times, I wanted to read again about the cracks appearing in serfdom, the agriculture crisis, the disease factors (especially the percentage numbers for Italy in decimation from Plague). And also and most importantly, the change in the Papal and Nobel rationalizations toward rights and preroga</span>
  <span id="freeText4779167864699282362" style="display:none">This is just a reaction for the 4 or 5 chapter revisit I made back to this excellent history. Having read the entire decades ago, and also having used it for some reference referrals for others at different times, I wanted to read again about the cracks appearing in serfdom, the agriculture crisis, the disease factors (especially the percentage numbers for Italy in decimation from Plague). And also and most importantly, the change in the Papal and Nobel rationalizations toward rights and prerogatives. This all becomes of high interest when reading numerous books of the 15th Century European Renaissance, which I have in the last 4 months. It intrigued me that nation systems or loyalty oaths could change that much in just 80 or 100 years. It wasn't just art, science, politico that changed- it was more. And yet there was no electricity, or vast power source or huge physical changes of living arrangement for the average human that changed. But reading this entire picture of overview for the 14th Century- you can see why nearly all the old restraints were breaking down. So much so that not even the horrific punishments of the 15th Century "disloyal" could buck the trend toward a different worldview.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4779167864699282362" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58734595" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58734595" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58734595?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58734595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10105063604566483594">I still remember reading this book when it first was published. It is another readable and accessible history by that great non-academic historian Barbara Tuchman. I first encountered her work when I read <i>The Proud Tower</i> so my expectations were high. That they were exceeded suggests that this is a work to which I should return as I seldom do for non-fiction. In this ambitious book she explores the tragedy, political intrigue and occasional dark comedy that surround the infestation of the Black P</span>
  <span id="freeText10105063604566483594" style="display:none">I still remember reading this book when it first was published. It is another readable and accessible history by that great non-academic historian Barbara Tuchman. I first encountered her work when I read <i>The Proud Tower</i> so my expectations were high. That they were exceeded suggests that this is a work to which I should return as I seldom do for non-fiction. In this ambitious book she explores the tragedy, political intrigue and occasional dark comedy that surround the infestation of the Black Plague in medieval Europe. She tells the tale from the perspective of a rather obscure French nobleman (Enguerrand de Coucy VII), a fresh approach and one that is rewarded well. The book is steeped with arresting details. The Black Death chapter is fascinating, and in her introduction Tuchman makes some interesting comparisons between medieval and latter-day behavior. History truly 'comes alive' with Tuchman's telling and that says it all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10105063604566483594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer49774228061456794">The Calamitous 14th Century: a time of war, class struggle, taxation, endless litigation, ravaging disease, religious intolerance, Christian versus Moslem, feckless leaders, plenty of lust, torture, self-interest -- 'a distant mirror' indeed. We are not so different. Look back or just look around.<br/><br/>And that is the point, I think, of this wonderful work of history and <u>literature</u>. Tuchman's wit and erudition are on full display.<br/><br/>Sometimes the reading went very slow, but only because it all seemed so </span>
  <span id="freeText49774228061456794" style="display:none">The Calamitous 14th Century: a time of war, class struggle, taxation, endless litigation, ravaging disease, religious intolerance, Christian versus Moslem, feckless leaders, plenty of lust, torture, self-interest -- 'a distant mirror' indeed. We are not so different. Look back or just look around.<br /><br />And that is the point, I think, of this wonderful work of history and <u>literature</u>. Tuchman's wit and erudition are on full display.<br /><br />Sometimes the reading went very slow, but only because it all seemed so important. This is History of the highest rank.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_450222111" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/450222111" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13485675-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349726589p2/13485675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/450222111?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/13485675-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13485675-beth?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13485675-beth?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13485675-beth?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13485675-beth?shelf=european-history-and-culture">european-history-and-culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13485675-beth?shelf=middle-ages">middle-ages</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer450222111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12370345730011894130">I remember noticing this book as a kid, before I knew the meaning of the word “calamitous.” It was sitting on one of my Dad’s bookshelves, and I found myself intrigued by the title, mentally picturing an ornate enchanted mirror that reflected images from far off centuries. <br/><br/>While I may have initially picked this book up because of a nostalgic childhood memory, I’m glad that I did. I knew so little about the 14th century before delving into its pages. I suppose I could have told you that it was th</span>
  <span id="freeText12370345730011894130" style="display:none">I remember noticing this book as a kid, before I knew the meaning of the word “calamitous.” It was sitting on one of my Dad’s bookshelves, and I found myself intrigued by the title, mentally picturing an ornate enchanted mirror that reflected images from far off centuries. <br /><br />While I may have initially picked this book up because of a nostalgic childhood memory, I’m glad that I did. I knew so little about the 14th century before delving into its pages. I suppose I could have told you that it was the century when the plague broke out in Europe, but little else. The Battle of Poitiers, the Black Prince, a ransomed king (as in "a king's ransom"!), peasant revolts, misguided crusaders, bands of roving mercenaries, eccentric noblemen, the seeds of the future protestant reformation, a king trying to rule amid fits of insanity –- all of these had previously escaped my notice. I found it a very engaging and accessible read: denser than, say, David McCullough, but not at all laborious.<br /><br />When an author tackles a subject as immense as an entire century, I’m always curious as to the organization of the book and how it will hang together. The thread that unifies this work is the life of Enguerrand de Coucy VII, a nobleman who played a central role in the political events of the day. I found it to be a good choice: not a king, but still someone involved in national upheavals of the time. And while much of the narrative focuses on key social and political figures, Ms. Tuchman also opens windows into the lives and perspectives of ordinary people of the day. <br /><br />The mirror may not have been enchanted, but I am glad to have finally peered in to see reflections of this far off century. <br /><br />*****<br /><br />If you appreciated this review, check out my blog at <a href="https://pagesandmargins.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">pagesandmargins.wordpress.com</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12370345730011894130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10318422689602582749">This is the second time I have 'read' this on audio. The first time was on tape, so, a long time ago.<br/><br/>I had to downgrade my opinion of this book on a second reading. Based on an attempt to read The Proud Tower, I realized that Barbara Tuchman could barely grasp the inner motives of 19th C. people, which made it less likely that she really understood 14th C. people. <br/><br/>Although she doesn't make clear ever why she regarded the 14th C. as 'a distant mirror,' I am inclined to guess that she thought the </span>
  <span id="freeText10318422689602582749" style="display:none">This is the second time I have 'read' this on audio. The first time was on tape, so, a long time ago.<br /><br />I had to downgrade my opinion of this book on a second reading. Based on an attempt to read The Proud Tower, I realized that Barbara Tuchman could barely grasp the inner motives of 19th C. people, which made it less likely that she really understood 14th C. people. <br /><br />Although she doesn't make clear ever why she regarded the 14th C. as 'a distant mirror,' I am inclined to guess that she thought the debacle at Nicopolis, when the French were defeated by Bajazet, was like unto the defeat of the U.S. in Vietnam. <br /><br />Thank God she didn't spell that out explicitly, or else I would have given the book two stars, despite some impressive narration and assemblage of facts.<br /><br />I have no complaints about the narration of Nadia May.</span>

<div id="review_2442396861" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2442396861" />
    <a title="Elliot" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13487368-elliot"><img alt="Elliot" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555808089p2/13487368.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2442396861?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elliot" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elliot" href="/user/show/13487368-elliot">Elliot</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394400266" href="/book/show/369050.A_Distant_Mirror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13487368-elliot?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13487368-elliot?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13487368-elliot?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2442396861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14890585093671832678">Illuminating is the most accurate word I can think of to describe this book. Tuchman's extensive history of the fourteenth century reveals an entirely new world to the modern reader. It was fascinating to learn about the daily habits of nobles and peasants alike, astonishing to learn how much money was idly wasted, and intriguing to learn about the hypocrisy of chivalry and the Church. As Tuchman progresses through the century, it becomes evermore painful to see the same destructive patterns bei</span>
  <span id="freeText14890585093671832678" style="display:none">Illuminating is the most accurate word I can think of to describe this book. Tuchman's extensive history of the fourteenth century reveals an entirely new world to the modern reader. It was fascinating to learn about the daily habits of nobles and peasants alike, astonishing to learn how much money was idly wasted, and intriguing to learn about the hypocrisy of chivalry and the Church. As Tuchman progresses through the century, it becomes evermore painful to see the same destructive patterns being repeated over and over again. Truly, calamitous is an apt adjective to describe the fourteenth century.<br /><br />There were a few aspects of this book that I didn't particularly like. Primarily, Tuchman uses a single character, Enguerrand de Coucy, "to narrow the focus to a manageable area..." (xiv). This strategy piqued my interest, but as the book went on, I began to dislike the increasing focus on Coucy. Certainly he was an important figure in that time, but I felt like there was too much emphasis on what he did and how great he was. In short, I feel like Tuchman's use of him as a guide detracted from, rather than augmented the narrative. Another complaint I have is that this book is too focused on France and, to a lesser extent, England and Italy. I learned almost nothing about Spain and only slightly more about the Holy Roman Empire and Central Europe. <br /><br />This narrative history certainly covers plenty of historical ground and provides a vivid look at the heart of the Middle Ages. If you ever want to learn about this time period, then this book would probably be of great interest and use to you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14890585093671832678" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xM5LOsh7M+rr855FkwyADL3/jLVhtyz8YrITxeVOjWL+6CLocyX6gkzYKiRk+YrTa6Brfa86ops5zFV0KA1/+A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tHfKOgrwfamoeCfoD1lw3ORAYMwHzdZtN9OWzCUQQuKOUaPosa60wQ9Tk4n4rHoDMh+HBMlAWApsrdB96FOweA==')})
  }
